  • The simpler and more obvious the discovery, the less equipped we are to figure it out by complicated methods — the significant can only be revealed through practice
  • Governments and universities have done very little for innovation and discovery, precisely because they look for the complicated, the grandiose, the scientistic — rarely for the wheel on the suitcase. Simplicity does not lead to laurels

Mind the Gaps

  • “Translational gap“: the time difference between formal discovery and first implementation — shown by Contopoulos-Ioannidis to be lengthening in modern times
  • Two centuries between discovery of germs and acceptance as cause of disease; thirty years between germ theory and antisepsis; sixty years between antisepsis and drug therapy
  • In the dark ages of medicine, doctors relied on balance of humors, continuing bloodletting and cataplasms for centuries after Harvey demonstrated blood circulation in the 1620s
  • Resisting old harmful technology is irrational; resisting new untested technology is rational risk management. But the resistance to implementing obvious improvements (removing harmful practices) is cowardice, not wisdom

Search and How Errors Can Be Investments

  • Trial and error is not truly random — it requires rationality in recognizing the favorable outcome and knowing what to discard
  • Every trial, every failure provides additional information — if you know what doesn’t work, you’re closer to what does. From the trial that fails to deliver, figure out progressively where to go
  • Greg Stemm (shipwreck treasure hunter): his “bad quarter“ (searching, no finds) was not distress but investment. His method: systematic elimination of search areas, each failure incrementally raising the probability of the next search yielding a result
  • Same method applies to oil and gas exploration — except the upside is nearly unlimited (vs. fixed treasure value)
  • Optionality-driven search is not foolishly random — it becomes tamed and harvested randomness

Creative and Uncreative Destructions

  • Joseph Schumpeter got a minor version of the point: creative destruction — some things need to break for the system to improve
  • But Schumpeter didn’t think in terms of uncertainty and opacity; he was completely smoked by interventionism, thinking governments could innovate by fiat
  • Both Schumpeter and his Harvard detractors missed the notion of antifragility as asymmetry (optionality) effects — the philosopher’s stone that is the real agent of growth

The Soviet-Harvard Department of Ornithology

  • Two types of knowledge: (1) apophatic — tacit, cannot be expressed in direct language, a way of doing things we can’t codify; (2) academic — explainable, formalizable, theoretizable, Harvardifiable, Sovietizable
  • The error of naive rationalism: overestimating the role of academic knowledge and degrading the uncodifiable, intuitive, experience-based type
  • Most texts define technology as “the application of scientific knowledge to practical projects“ — this leads us to believe knowledge flows from lofty science down to lowly practice. The reality is largely the reverse
  • The Baconian linear model: Academia Applied Science and Technology Practice. While valid in rare cases (the atomic bomb), the exact reverse is true in most domains examined
  • Lecturing birds on how to fly: imagine hieratic professors lecturing birds on flight. The bird flies. They rush to claim the bird obeyed them. The Harvard Department of Ornithology is now “indispensable“ for bird flying and gets government research funds
  • Birds write no papers or books, so we never get their side of the story. The priests keep broadcasting theirs

Epiphenomena

  • The Soviet-Harvard illusion belongs to a class of causal illusions called epiphenomena: when you stand on a bridge with a large compass, you develop the impression the compass is directing the ship rather than merely reflecting its direction
  • We see high academic research in wealthy, developed countries and uncritically conclude research generates the wealth — a classic epiphenomenal error
  • Greed as a cause: whenever an economic crisis occurs, greed is blamed. But greed is much older than systemic fragility — it existed since Virgil’s auri sacra fames. Greed is an epiphenomenon; the real cause is the system’s fragility
  • “Lack of vigilance“ is similarly proposed as a cause — but that’s like saying the cause of a mafia don’s death is making enemies, and the cure is making friends

Debunking Epiphenomena

  • Granger causality (Clive Granger, Economics Nobel): the only rigorous scientific technique for establishing causation — look at the sequence of events (what comes first, A or B?) rather than just co-occurrence
  • Life is lived forward but remembered backward (Kierkegaard) — books exacerbate this, creating illusions of causality from scrambled sequences
  • Cherry-picking has optionality: the one telling the story can show only confirmatory examples and ignore the rest. The more volatility and dispersion, the rosier the best story (and darker the worst)

The Alternative Process

  • The real process is a loop: Random Tinkering (antifragile) Heuristics (technology) Practice and Apprenticeship Random Tinkering ...
  • In parallel: Practice Academic Theories Academic Theories Academic Theories ... (with rare accidental leaks, overhyped and grossly generalized)

Chapter 14: When Two Things Are Not the “Same Thing“

Green lumber another “blue“ — Where we look for the arrow of discovery — Putting Iraq in the middle of Pakistan — Prometheus never looked back

The Abu Dhabi Model

  • Abu Dhabi: oil-rich countries building huge universities, importing Sorbonne and NYU, under the postulation that oil reserves can be turned into knowledge — the belief that university knowledge generates economic wealth
  • But this comes from superstition, not empiricism. Switzerland (Chapter 5) — a place with very low formal education — is among the wealthiest
  • The spending on imported education is completely sterile — a great transfer of resources rather than milking antifragility by forcing citizens to make money naturally through circumstances

Where Are the Stressors?

  • Necessitas magistra (Erasmus): “Necessity is the mother of invention.“ Publilius Syrus: hominem experiri multa paupertas iubet — “poverty makes experiences“
  • Taleb’s village of Amioun, pillaged and evacuated during the war, became opulent twenty-five years later. “People from Amioun only do well when shaken.“ That’s antifragility

L’Art pour l’Art, to Learn for Learning’s Sake

  • Lant Pritchet (World Bank economist): no evidence that raising the general level of education raises income at the country level. The opposite is true — wealth leads to the rise of education, not the other way
  • Ha-Joon Chang: in 1960 Taiwan had lower literacy than the Philippines and half the income; today Taiwan has ten times the income. Korea had lower literacy than Argentina; today it has three times as much
  • Sub-Saharan Africa saw markedly increasing literacy rates accompanied with a decrease in standard of living
  • The fooled by randomness effect: rich countries are educated people infer education makes countries rich (mistaking association for causation)
  • For individuals, education builds helpful credentials — but this effect washes out at the country level
  • Alison Wolf: the simple one-way relationship “education spending in, economic growth out“ simply doesn’t exist. Egypt’s giant leap in education did not translate into growth
“Scholarship and organized education are not the same.“

Polished Dinner Partners

  • In ancient times, learning was for learning’s sake — making someone a good person worth talking to, not to increase gold in the city’s coffers
  • The halo effect: skills in conversation/culture are mistakenly assumed to translate into business/practical skills. The more cultured and interesting someone is, the more we’re trapped into thinking they’re effective
  • Skills at doing skills at talking: good practitioners are often totally incomprehensible — they don’t waste energy on elegant narratives. Entrepreneurs are doers, not thinkers. The quality lies in their product, not their conversation
  • Bureaucrats, lacking objective success metrics and market forces, are selected on the “halo effects“ of shallow looks and elegance

The Green Lumber Fallacy

  • From the book What I Learned Losing a Million Dollars: Joe Siegel, one of the most successful lumber traders, thought “green lumber“ was lumber painted green (it’s actually freshly cut, undried lumber). He made it his profession to trade it
  • Meanwhile, the narrator was into grand intellectual theories about commodity prices — and went bust
  • The successful expert was ignorant of central matters like the color designation “green“ — but he knew things about lumber that nonexperts think are unimportant. People we call ignorant might not be ignorant
  • Predicting price flow in lumber had little to do with the narrative details outsiders assume are important. Evolution does not rely on narratives
  • The green lumber fallacy: the situation in which one mistakes a source of necessary knowledge — the “greenness“ of lumber — for another, less visible from the outside, less tractable, less narratable

Taleb’s Discovery of the Green Lumber Fallacy

  • When Taleb became a derivatives professional, foreign exchange traders were street-level New Jersey/Brooklyn Italian fellows — not economists or geopoliticians. They couldn’t find Switzerland on a map, yet were the world’s best Swiss franc traders
  • London traders were even more sub-blue collar — East End cockney, using their own numbering system (“Lady Godiva“ = five, “ching“ = fifteen, “pony“ = twenty-five)
  • Price and reality as seen by economists are not the same thing — the relation may have optionality in places that non-sentence-savvy people knew deep inside

How Fat Tony Got Rich (and Fat)

  • Fat Tony made eighteen million dollars from three hundred thousand in the aftermath of the Kuwait war (January 1991)
  • Every analyst predicted oil would rise with war. Tony bet against it — prices had already adjusted to expectations. “It has to be ’in the price’“
  • Oil collapsed from $39 to almost half. Many people lost their shirts while correctly predicting war — they confused the event with its price effect. “Kuwait and oil are not the same ting“
  • Fund managers with elaborate war-room analysis of Kuwait, Iraq, Washington knew everything except the simple fact that it had nothing to do with oil — not the same “ting“
“Good speculative bets come to you, you don’t get them by just staying focused on the news.“

Conflation

  • There is something (perception, ideas, theories) and a function of something (price, reality). The conflation is to mistake one for the other, forgetting the function has different properties
  • The more asymmetries between the something and the function of something, the more difference between the two — they may end up having nothing to do with each other
  • Jim Simons (Renaissance Technologies) escaped the conflation: he never hires economists or finance people, only physicists and mathematicians doing pattern recognition — accessing the internal logic without theorizing. He never listens to economists or reads their reports
  • Ariel Rubinstein (game theory leader) gets the green lumber fallacy: he refuses to claim his theoretical knowledge translates into anything directly practical. Economics is like a fable — to stimulate ideas, not to direct practice
  • Rubinstein tried applying game theory bargaining to a Levantine souk vendor — the vendor told him “For generations, we have bargained in our way and you come and try to change it?“ Theory should stay independent from practice
  • Specialization (Ricardian comparative advantage) blows up when imposed top-down by policy makers, but works well when reached progressively through evolutionary means with buffers and layers of redundancy
  • The important things that cannot be easily narrated lie mainly in optionality — the missed optionality of things. You don’t go to school to learn optionality; you go to school to become blind to it

Prometheus and Epimetheus

  • Prometheus = “fore-thinker“ — represents the progress of civilization. Epimetheus = “after-thinker“ — falls for retrospective narrative distortion
  • Optionality is Promethean: reversible, benign mistakes. Narratives are Epimethean: the gravity and irreversibility of Pandora’s box
  • Table 4 contrasts narrative knowledge vs. optionality-driven tinkering across many dimensions: tourist vs. flneur, concave vs. convex, fragile vs. robust rationality, trapped in a story vs. no meaningful dependence on story
  • Heuristic knowledge (rules of thumb) embedded in traditions: evolution operates on tacit, unexplainable rules transmitted through generations — what Karl Popper called evolutionary epistemology
  • An idea survives not because it is better than the competition, but because the person who holds it has survived. Grandmother’s wisdom should be empirically superior to business school knowledge
  • Expert problems: when you are fragile, you need to know a lot more than when you are antifragile. When you think you know more than you do, you are fragile to error

Chapter 15: History Written by the Losers

The birds may perhaps listen — Combining stupidity with wisdom rather than the opposite — Where we look for the arrow of discovery — A vindication of trial and error

The Lecturing-Birds-How-to-Fly Effect Across Domains

  • Table 5 shows the misattribution of results across fields:
  • Jet Engine: attributed to physicists, actually from tinkering engineers (busted by Scranton)
  • Architecture: attributed to Euclidean geometry, actually from heuristics and guild recipes (busted by Beaujouan)
  • Cybernetics: attributed to Norbert Wiener, actually from programmers “wiki-style“ (busted by Mindell)
  • Derivatives formulas: attributed to Black-Scholes-Merton, actually from traders and practitioners like Regnauld, Bachelier, Thorp (busted by Haug and Taleb)
  • Medicine: attributed to biological understanding, actually from luck, trial and error, side effects
  • Industrial Revolution: attributed to Scientific Revolution, actually from adventurers and hobbyists (busted by Kealey)

The Options Pricing Story

  • Taleb and Espen Haug (trader turned researcher) investigated the origin of option pricing formulas: traders had vastly, vastly more sophistication than the formula, at least a century before the academics claimed to derive it
  • Practitioners’ sophistication was picked up through natural selection, survivorship, apprenticeship, and experience
  • The real sequence: Traders trade traders figure out techniques academics find formulas and claim traders are using them new traders believe academics blowups (theory-induced fragility)
  • The Barnard College editor tried to rewrite their story in the Wiley Encyclopedia of Quantitative Finance — scientific history being made in the making
  • “We don’t put theories into practice. We create theories out of practice.“ — ex cura theoria nascitur

The Evidence Staring at Us

  • Phil Scranton (Rutgers professor): jet engines were built by trial-and-error experiential methods, without anyone understanding the theory. Builders needed engineers who knew how to twist things to make the engine work. Theory came later
  • David Mindell: cybernetics was not invented by Norbert Wiener — he was articulating ideas about feedback control that had long been in practice in engineering
  • Architecture: before the thirteenth century, no more than five persons in Europe could perform a division. Yet builders figured out resistance of materials — buildings still standing. Villard de Honnecourt documented how cathedrals were built: experimental heuristics, small tricks and rules, not formal geometry
  • Romans built aqueducts without mathematics (Roman numerals don’t even allow quantitative analysis). A side effect of mathematics may be making people over-optimize and cut corners, causing fragility
  • Vitruvius (De architectura): no mention of Euclid, mostly heuristics — master guiding apprentice knowledge

Is It Like Cooking?

  • Cooking is the perfect optionality business: add an ingredient, keep it if it works (agrees with Fat Tony’s taste buds), discard if not. Recipes evolved through generations of collective tinkering — entirely without conjectures about food chemistry
  • Pure physics is the exception where the “epistemic base“ genuinely matters (Higgs Boson, Le Verrier predicting Neptune). But most domains resemble cooking a lot more than physics
  • Medicine today remains an apprenticeship model with theoretical science in the background, made to look like science

The Computer and the Internet

  • Computers: combinatorics (basic science) computers. But first they were cumbersome boxes good for database management. Then the keyboard/screen. Then word processing (1980s). Then Phase Two: the Internet — from DARPA military resilience project personal computer social networks, dating. All unintended consequences
  • At no point did academic science serve in setting its direction — it served as a slave to chance discoveries by college dropouts and overgrown high school students

China’s Missing Mania

  • Joseph Needham showed China, as it became a top-down mandarinate (Soviet-Harvard centralized scribes), lost the “European mania for tinkering and improving.“ They had all the means to develop a spinning machine but “nobody tried“

The Industrial Revolution

  • Terence Kealey (The Economic Laws of Scientific Research): questions the “linear model“ that academic science leads to technology. Universities prospered as a consequence of national wealth, not the other way. Government-funded research has had iatrogenic effects — private investment decreases and moves away
  • The steam engine emerged from preexisting technology, created by uneducated, often isolated men applying practical common sense and intuition
  • Textile technologies (flying shuttle 1733, spinning jenny 1770): nothing to science — empirical developments by skilled craftsmen
  • Two main sources of innovation in the 19th-20th centuries: the hobbyist and the English rector (both in barbell situations — secure income + free time + optionality)
  • English parish priests with comfortable houses and scones: Rev. Cartwright (power loom), Rev. Jack Russell (bred the terrier), Rev. Buckland (first dinosaur authority), Rev. Greenwell (modern archaeology), Rev. Garrett (submarine), Rev. Gilbert White (naturalist)
“Luxe, calme et volupt“ — the solitary scholar in the tranquility of his spacious room, contrasted with the tumult of schools and collective workplaces

Governments Should Spend on Nonteleological Tinkering, Not Research

  • Government spending can work — the Internet came from DARPA. But functionaries are too teleological in how they spend. Big Pharma is its own enemy
  • Blue sky research: grants given to people, not projects, spread in small amounts across many researchers. Venture capitalists back entrepreneurs, not ideas — “bet on the jockey, not the horse“
  • The 1/N strategy: if you face n options, invest in all in equal small amounts. In Extremistan with power-law payoffs, it’s more important to be in something than to miss it. “The payoff can be so large that you can’t afford not to be in everything“

The Case in Medicine

  • The National Cancer Institute’s “war on cancer“ (Nixon, early 1970s): Morton Meyers documents that over twenty years of screening 144,000 plant extracts (15,000 species), not a single plant-based anticancer drug reached approved status — directed research failed. Meanwhile, the Vinca Alkaloids (major cancer drugs) were discovered by chance
  • Private industry develops nine drugs out of ten. Even of the 46 drugs with significant sales from NIH, only about three had anything to do with federal funding
  • When an academic researcher makes a chance discovery, he tends to disregard the consequences because it’s not what he was looking for — failing to exercise his option, a violation of rationality
  • Chemotherapy came from mustard gas (WWII ship bombing off Bari, Italy, 1942) — discovered by observing effects on soldiers’ blood cells. Churchill kept it secret; the Geneva Conventions banned it
  • James Le Fanu: the “therapeutic revolution“ (postwar period) was not ignited by scientific insight — it came from the realization that synthetic chemistry blindly and randomly would deliver remedies that had eluded doctors for centuries. The sulfonamides (Gerhard Domagk) are the central example
  • The increase in theoretical understanding (the “epistemic base“) came with a decrease in new drugs. In complex domains, “knowledge“ can inhibit research
  • Drug obliquity: aspirin changed its uses many times. Judah Folkman’s angiogenesis inhibitors led to macular degeneration treatment (bevacizumab/Avastin) — more effective than the original intent
  • Designer drugs have a problem: with tens of thousands of drugs on the market, the interactions between them are underestimated by a factor of four. The pool of available drugs should be shrinking, not growing

Matt Ridley’s Anti-Teleological Argument

  • Al-Ghazali (medieval skeptic philosopher, aka Algazel): came up with the metaphor of the pin (now falsely attributed to Adam Smith). The pin doesn’t have a single maker — twenty-five persons collaborate guided by an invisible hand, with no one knowing how to produce it alone
  • Matt Ridley: the difference between humans and animals lies in the ability to collaborate and let ideas “copulate.“ Collaboration is superadditive (1+1 >> 2). Since you cannot forecast or direct collaborations, you can only create an environment that facilitates them
  • Adam Smith meets Algazel: the invisible hand is the market; for the religious skeptic, it is God. Fideism (skepticism of human knowledge) is epistemologically equivalent to pure empirical skepticism
  • All great skeptics have been religious or pro-religion — skepticism of expert knowledge, not of abstract entities

Corporate Teleology

  • Strategic planning has no evidence of working — and evidence against it. William Starbuck debunked its effectiveness; it makes corporations option-blind
  • Almost everything theoretical in management (Taylorism, productivity stories) has been exposed as pseudoscience. Matthew Stewart (The Management Myth), Abrahamson and Friedman (A Perfect Mess)
  • Business drift: Coca-Cola began as a pharmaceutical product. Tiffany & Co. started as a stationery store. Raytheon (missile guidance) was a refrigerator maker. Nokia was a paper mill. DuPont (Teflon, Kevlar) started as an explosives company. Avon started in door-to-door book sales

The Inverse Turkey Problem

  • Evidence of absence is not absence of evidence: for the antifragile, good news tends to be absent from past data. For the fragile, it’s the bad news that doesn’t show
  • The Carlos Slim problem: if you go to Mexico and sample fifty million people, you may not include the one person (Slim) who is richer than the bottom seventy to ninety million combined. You massively underestimate total wealth
  • For antifragile cases (positive asymmetries, trial and error, biotech): the sample track record will tend to underestimate the long-term average; it will hide the qualities, not the defects
  • For fragile cases (negative asymmetries, turkey problems): the sample track record will tend to overestimate the long-term average; it will hide the defects and display the qualities
  • Gary Pisano (Harvard): looked at biotech and concluded it had pitiful potential because “most firms earn no profit.“ Classic inverse-turkey mistake — in Extremistan, most firms should earn no profit; the rare event dominates
  • Kenneth Froot (Harvard): opposite error — looked at reinsurance companies and thought they made too much profit, not realizing catastrophes seemed to occur less often than premia reflected. One single episode (asbestos) bankrupted families of Lloyd underwriters, losing income made over generations

To Fail Seven Times, Plus or Minus Two

  • Rules from the chapter: (i) Look for optionality and rank things accordingly; (ii) Preferably open-ended, not closed-ended payoffs; (iii) Invest in people, not business plans — look for someone capable of changing six or seven times (Marc Andreessen’s modus operandi); (iv) Make sure you are barbelled

The Charlatan, the Academic, and the Showman

  • The empirics — medical practitioners who relied on experiment and experience — have been treated badly by history. They didn’t write books. Formal thinkers wrote the history
  • “Charlatan“ was a synonym for empirick. “Empiric“ designated someone who relied on trial and error and tinkering — held to be inferior professionally, socially, and intellectually
  • The medical establishment regulated empirics out of economic competition (protecting their incomes). But “legitimate“ doctors were silently copying remedies developed by the Others
  • Formal academics were no better than charlatans in practice — they just hid their fraud under more convincing rationalizations. They were just organized quacks
  • “All that is nonrigorous is nonacademic“ does NOT imply “all that is nonacademic is nonrigorous“ — a logical fallacy used to dismiss practitioners

Chapter 16: A Lesson in Disorder

Where is the next street fight? — How to decommoditize, detouristify — The intelligent student (also in reverse) — Flneur as options

The Ecological and the Ludic

  • Two domains: the ludic (game-like, rules supplied in advance) and the ecological (real life, rules unknown, variables not isolable)
  • Skills acquired in a classroom are non-ecological — they stay largely in the classroom. Chess skills don’t improve general reasoning; blind chess players can’t remember things outside the board
  • Laura Martignon: children’s ability to count degrades right after being taught arithmetic. Children who don’t know arithmetic correctly figure there are fourteen intervals between fifteen poles; those who studied arithmetic get confused and say fifteen

The Touristification of the Soccer Mom

  • E. O. Wilson: the biggest hindrance to child development is the soccer mom — eliminating trial and error, antifragility, and the ecological from children’s lives, transforming them into nerds on preexisting maps of reality
  • One can be an intellectual without being a nerd: have a private library instead of a classroom, spend time as a rational flneur benefiting from randomness
  • We need randomness, mess, uncertainty, self-discovery, near-traumatic episodes — the right type of rigor — compared to the structured, fake life of an empty-suit CEO
  • Lions in captivity live longer and are technically richer — but they are not lions. Only the autodidacts are free
“We do not study for life, but only for the lecture room.“ — Seneca (non vitae, sed scolae discimus)

An Antifragile (Barbell) Education

  • Taleb’s father was known in Lebanon as the “Intelligent Student Student Intelligent“ — taleb nagib is Arabic for “intelligent student.“ He was national valedictorian
  • Observing his father: being an Intelligent Student meant mostly in the negative — things intelligent students were unable to understand. Some blindness came with the package
  • Good grades carry side effects — they correspond to intellectual sacrifice. The person at the exact bottom of his father’s class turned out to be the most successful (self-made merchant with a huge yacht)
  • Taleb was a barbell autodidact: studied the exact minimum necessary to pass exams, read voraciously outside the curriculum — thirty to sixty hours per week of self-directed reading
  • The trick is to be bored with a specific book, not with reading itself. Jump from one book to the next when bored. The number of pages absorbed grows faster. Trial and error is freedom
  • At Wharton, Taleb specialized in probability and rare events: “I saw the limits of probability in front of me, clear as crystal, but could not find the words to express the point.“ He ordered every book with “probability“ or “stochastic“ in its title and read for years
  • “Much of what other people know isn’t worth knowing.“ The treasure, what one needs to know for a profession, lies outside the corpus, as far from the center as possible
  • What I was given to study in school I have forgotten; what I decided to read on my own, I still remember

Chapter 17: Fat Tony Debates Socrates

Piety for the impious — Fat Tony does not drink milk — Always ask poets to explain their poetry — Mystagogue philosophaster

Euthyphro

  • Fat Tony believes they were totally justified in putting Socrates to death
  • This chapter completes the discussion of the difference between narrated, intelligible knowledge and the more opaque kind that is entirely probed by tinkering — the two columns of Table 4 separating narrative and non-narrative action
  • There is this error of thinking that things always have a reason that is accessible to us — that we can comprehend easily
  • The most severe mistake made in life is to mistake the unintelligible for the unintelligent — something Nietzsche figured out. It resembles the turkey problem, mistaking what we don’t see for the nonexistent
  • Plato expressed himself chiefly through his use of Socrates, the Athenian who became the most influential philosopher in the modern sense. Socrates left no writing of his own — we get direct representation mainly through Plato and Xenophon
  • In one of Plato’s dialogues, Euthyphro, Socrates was outside the courthouse awaiting his trial when Euthyphro, a religious expert, struck up a conversation. Socrates started explaining the charges against him (corrupting the youth, introducing new gods)
  • Euthyphro was on his way to charge his own father with manslaughter — Socrates started wondering how this was compatible with Euthyphro’s religious duties
  • Socrates’ technique: make his interlocutor agree to a thesis, then proceed to show how the statements he agreed to are inconsistent with the original thesis — establishing that the person has no clue what he’s talking about
  • Euthyphro could not produce a definition of piety. The dialogue continued with more definitions until Euthyphro found a polite excuse to run away — the reader is left with the impression it could have gone on for twenty-five centuries without bringing us any closer to anything

Fat Tony Versus Socrates

  • Imagine Fat Tony asked by Socrates how he defined piety — Fat Tony’s answer would have been most certainly to get lost
  • Fat Tony’s power in life is that he never lets the other person frame the question — he taught Nero that an answer is planted in every question; never respond with a straight answer to a question that makes no sense to you
FAT TONY: “You are asking me to define what characteristic makes a difference between pious and nonpious. Do I really need to be able to tell you what it is to be able to conduct a pious action?“
SOCRATES: “How can you use a word like ’piety’ without knowing what it means?“
FAT TONY: “Do I actually have to tell you in plain barbarian non-Greek English what it means to prove that I know and understand what it means? I don’t know it in words but I know what it is.“
  • Fat Tony would have taken Socrates down his own road, using the Socratic pattern of dialogue:
FAT TONY: “Tell me, old man. Does a child need to define mother’s milk to understand the need to drink it?“
SOCRATES: “No, he does not need to.“
FAT TONY: “And my dear Socrates, does a dog need to define what an owner is to be loyal to him?“
SOCRATES (puzzled): “A dog has instinct. It does not reflect on its life.“
FAT TONY: “But are we humans so fundamentally different as to be completely stripped of instinct leading us to do things we have no clue about? Do we have to limit life to what we can answer in proto-Brooklyn English?“
  • Without waiting for Socrates’ answer (only suckers wait for answers; questions are not made for answers):
FAT TONY: “Then, my good Socrates, why do you think that we need to fix the meaning of things?“
SOCRATES: “My dear Mega-Tony, we need to know what we are talking about when we talk about things... An unexamined life is not worth living.“
FAT TONY: “The problem, my poor old Greek, is that you are killing the things we can know but not express. And if I asked someone riding a bicycle just fine to give me the theory behind his bicycle riding, he would fall from it. By bullying and questioning people you confuse them and hurt them.“
  • Then, looking at him patronizingly, with a smirk, very calmly:
FAT TONY: “My dear Socrates... you know why they are putting you to death? It is because you make people feel stupid for blindly following habits, instincts, and traditions. You may be occasionally right. But you may confuse them about things they’ve been doing just fine without getting in trouble. You are destroying people’s illusions about themselves. You are taking the joy of ignorance out of the things we don’t understand. And you have no answer; you have no answer to offer them.“

Primacy of Definitional Knowledge

  • What Fat Tony is hitting here is the very core of philosophy: the main questions that became philosophy were first raised by Socrates — “what is existence?,“ “what are morals?,“ “what is a proof?,“ “what is science?“
  • The question in Euthyphro pervades the various dialogues written by Plato — Socrates is seeking relentlessly definitions of the essential nature of the thing concerned rather than descriptions of the properties by means of which we can recognize them
  • Socrates cross-examined the poets and reported they had no more clue than the public about their own works — in Plato’s Apology he recounted taking them elaborate passages and asking what was the meaning
  • This priority of definitional knowledge led to Plato’s thesis: you cannot know anything unless you know the Forms — if we cannot define piety from working with particulars, start with the universals. If you cannot get a map from a territory, build a territory out of the map
  • In defense of Socrates: if his questions could not define what something was, they at least allowed him to be certain about what a thing was not

Mistaking the Unintelligible for the Unintelligent

  • Fat Tony had many precursors who attacked the primacy of philosophy and rationalistic knowledge — many forgotten because structured learning likes the impoverishment and simplification of naive rationalism, easy to teach
  • A vivid modern attack came from the young Friedrich Nietzsche — his first book, The Birth of Tragedy, written in his early twenties, went after Socrates, whom he called “mystagogue of science,“ for “making existence appear comprehensible“
  • This brilliant passage exposes what Taleb calls the sucker-rationalistic fallacy: “Perhaps — thus he [Socrates] should have asked himself — what is not intelligible to me is not necessarily unintelligent? Perhaps there is a realm of wisdom from which the logician is exiled?“
“What is not intelligible to me is not necessarily unintelligent“ — perhaps the most potent sentence in all of Nietzsche’s century
  • Nietzsche is allergic to Socrates’ version of truth: according to Socrates, one does not knowingly do evil — knowledge is the panacea; error is evil; hence science is an optimistic enterprise. It is the very goodness of knowledge that Nietzsche questioned
  • In The Birth of Tragedy, Nietzsche sees two forces: the Apollonian (measured, balanced, rational, imbued with reason and self-restraint) and the Dionysian (dark, visceral, wild, untamed, hard to understand). Ancient Greek culture balanced the two, until Socrates’ influence gave a larger share to the Apollonian — yang without yin
  • Nietzsche figured out antifragility: while many attribute “creative destruction“ to Schumpeter, the more erudite source is Karl Marx, but it is indeed Nietzsche who first coined the term with reference to Dionysus — “creatively destructive“ and “destructively creative“
  • Reading The Birth of Tragedy twice — as a child and after a life thinking of randomness — Taleb realized Nietzsche understood that growth in knowledge cannot proceed without the Dionysian. Stochastic tinkering can be the source, and the Apollonian can be part of the selection process

Tradition

  • As Fat Tony said, Socrates was put to death because he disrupted something that was working just fine. Things are too complicated to be expressed in words; by doing so, you kill humans — or people may be focusing on the right things but we are not good enough to figure it out intellectually
  • Death and martyrdom make good marketing — Socrates’ classical critics believed he was destroying the foundations of society — the heuristics transmitted by the elders
  • Cato the Elder was highly allergic to Socrates. He had the bottom-line mind of Fat Tony, with a much higher civic sense, respect for tradition, and commitment to moral rectitude. Plutarch quotes him: “Socrates was a mighty babbler who tried to make himself tyrant of his country in order to destroy its customs and entice its citizens into holding views contrary to law and order“
  • Many defended this different type of knowledge: Edmund Burke countered the French Revolution for disrupting the “collected reasons of the ages,“ advocating small trial-and-error experiments in social systems. Michael Oakeshott believed traditions provide filtered collective knowledge. Joseph de Maistre was a counter-Enlightenment thinker who believed in the fundamental depravity of men unless checked by some dictatorship
  • Wittgenstein would be at the top of modern antifragile thinkers — his remarkable insight into the inexpressible with words, the green lumber issue. He sacrificed everything for philosophy
  • Friedrich Hayek opposed social planning on the grounds that the pricing system reveals knowledge embedded in society, not accessible to a social planner. But Hayek missed the notion of optionality as a substitute for the social planner — he believed in distributed intelligence, not optionality as a replacement for intelligence
  • Claude Lvi-Strauss showed that nonliterate peoples had their own “science of the concrete,“ a holistic way of thinking in terms of objects and their sensuous qualities — rich as and even richer than ours. Green lumber
  • John Gray fights the prevailing idea that the Enlightenment is a panacea, showing how what we call scientific progress can be a mirage

The Sucker-Nonsucker Distinction

  • For Fat Tony, the distinction in life isn’t True or False, but sucker or nonsucker. In real life, as we saw with Seneca and Thales, exposure is more important than knowledge; decision effects supersede logic
  • Textbook “knowledge“ misses a dimension: the hidden asymmetry of benefits — the need to focus on the payoff from your actions instead of studying the structure of the world
Philosophers talk about truth and falsehood. People in life talk about payoff, exposure, and consequences (risks and rewards), hence fragility and antifragility.
  • True and False (“belief“) play a poor, secondary role in human decisions; it is the payoff from the True and the False that dominates — and it is almost always asymmetric, with one consequence much bigger than the other

Fragility, Not Probability

  • We check people for weapons before they board the plane — not because we believe they are terrorists (False), but because we are fragile to terrorism. There is an asymmetry: the payoff of the True (that they are terrorists) is too large
  • You decide principally based on fragility, not probability. You decide principally based on fragility, not so much on True/False.
  • If you jotted down all the decisions you’ve taken over your lifetime, you would realize almost all had asymmetric payoff — one side carrying a larger consequence. True/False (probability) does not work in the real world; it is the payoff that matters
  • A 95 percent confidence level tells you the result being wrong is only 5% likely — but this ignores the size of the effects. If you told someone a plane was safe with 95 percent confidence level, that 1 percent crash probability would be alarming
  • You have taken probably a billion decisions in your life. How many times have you computed probabilities? In casinos, but not elsewhere

Conflation of Events and Exposure

  • This brings us again to the green lumber fallacy: a Black Swan event and how it affects you — its impact on your finances, emotions, the destruction it will cause — are not the same “ting“
  • The predictors’ reply when we point out their failures has typically been “we need better computation“ to predict the event better — instead of the vastly more effective “modify your exposure“ and learn to get out of trouble
  • Something religions and traditional heuristics have been better at enforcing than naive and cosmetic science

Conclusion to Book IV

  • This section has attempted to vindicate the unreasonable mavericks, engineers, freelance entrepreneurs, innovative artists, and anti-academic thinkers — some had great courage, not just the courage to put forth their ideas, but the courage to accept to live in a world they knew they did not understand. And they enjoyed it
  • Doing is wiser than you are prone to believe — and more rational. What was done here is to debunk the Lecturing-Birds-How-to-Fly epiphenomenon and the “linear model,“ using the simple mathematical properties of optionality, which does not require knowledge or intelligence, merely rationality in choice
  • No empirical evidence supports the statement that organized research leads to the great things promised by universities — the promoters of the Soviet-Harvard idea do not use optionality or second-order effects, which invalidates their views about the role of teleological science

What Will Happen Next?

  • When Taleb last met Alison Wolf, they discussed the dire problem with education — Ivy League universities becoming a status luxury good, like a Vuitton bag or a Cartier watch
  • It is a huge drag on middle-class parents who have been plowing an increased share of their savings into these institutions — transferring their money to administrators, real estate developers, professors, and agents
  • In the United States, there is a buildup of student loans that parallels the subprime-type buildup — both central to the formation of a bubble

Book V: The Nonlinear and the Nonlinear

  • Book V presents a more technical rephrasing of the definition of antifragility from Seneca’s asymmetry
  • The nontechnical reader can skip Book V without any loss — the definition of antifragility from Seneca’s asymmetry is amply sufficient for a literary read of the rest of the book

Chapter 18: On the Difference Between a Large Stone and a Thousand Pebbles

How to punish with a stone — I landed early (once) — Why attics are always useful — On the great benefits of avoiding Heathrow unless you have a guitar

Taleb’s Return to Nonlinearity

  • The Procrustean bed in life consists precisely in simplifying the nonlinear and making it linear — the simplification that distorts
  • Taleb’s interest in the nonlinearity of exposures went away as he began dealing with other matters related to uncertainty — the nature of randomness rather than how things react to random events
  • After the crisis of the late 2000s, Taleb went through an episode of hell owing to contact with the press — suddenly deintellectualized, corrupted, extracted from his habitat, propelled into being a public commodity
  • He found it hard for members of the media and public to accept that the job of a scholar is to ignore insignificant current affairs, to write books, not emails, and not to give lectures dancing on a stage
  • Taleb then opted out of public life — when he managed to retake control of his schedule and brain, Lady Fortuna brought two ideas to him that had been inside him all along
  • The tools of analysis of nonlinear effects are quite universal — he realized that everything nonlinear around him could be subjected to the same techniques of detection

A Simple Rule to Detect the Fragile

  • A story from the rabbinical literature (Midrash Tehillim), probably from earlier Near Eastern lore: A king, angry at his son, swore to crush him with a large stone. After calming down, he realized he was in trouble — a king who breaks his oath is unfit to rule. His sage advisor came up with a solution: have the stone cut into very small pebbles and pelt the son with them
  • The difference between a thousand pebbles and a large stone of equivalent weight is a potent illustration of how fragility stems from nonlinear effects
  • Nonlinear means the response is not straightforward and not a straight line — if you double the dose, you get a lot more or a lot less than double the effect
  • Throwing a ten-pound stone at someone’s head will cause more than twice the harm of a five-pound stone, more than five times the harm of a one-pound stone — if you draw a line on a graph with harm on the vertical axis and size of stone on the horizontal, it will be curved, not a straight line
  • This is a refinement of asymmetry
For the fragile, shocks bring higher harm as their intensity increases (up to a certain level).
  • Examples of nonlinear harm:
  • Driving a car into a wall at 50 mph causes more damage than driving into the same wall ten times at 5 mph — the harm at 50 mph is more than ten times the harm at 5 mph
  • Drinking seven bottles of wine (Bordeaux) in one sitting is more harmful than drinking one bottle a day for seven days — every additional glass harms you more than the preceding one, hence the system is fragile to alcoholic consumption
  • Letting a porcelain cup drop from one foot (about 30 cm) is worse than twelve times the damage from a drop of one inch (2.5 cm)
  • Jumping from thirty feet (ten meters) brings more than ten times the harm of jumping from three feet (one meter) — thirty feet seems to be the cutoff point for death from free fall
  • This is a simple expansion of the foundational asymmetry from Seneca’s thinking — asymmetry is necessarily nonlinearity: more harm than benefits means an increase in intensity brings more harm than a corresponding decrease offers benefits

Why Is Fragility Nonlinear?

  • The central argument: fragility is generally in the nonlinear and not in the linear — the intuition from the porcelain cup
  • The answer has to do with the structure of survival probabilities: conditional on something being unharmed (or having survived), it is more harmed by a single large rock than a thousand pebbles — by a single large infrequent event than by the cumulative effect of smaller shocks
  • If jumping one millimeter caused an exact linear fraction of the damage of jumping thirty feet, we would already be dead from cumulative harm — we would have expired within hours from touching objects or pacing in our living rooms
  • The fragility that comes from linearity is immediately visible (the object would already be broken), so we rule it out
  • What is fragile is something that is both unbroken and subjected to nonlinear effects — and extreme, rare events, since large impacts of large size (or high speed) are rarer than ones of small size (and slow speed)
  • In connection with Black Swans and extreme events: there are a lot more ordinary events than extreme events — in financial markets, at least ten thousand times more events of 0.1% magnitude than events of 10% magnitude
  • Close to eight thousand microearthquakes daily on planet Earth (below 2 on the Richter scale) — about three million a year, totally harmless; but shocks of intensity 6 and higher make the newspapers
  • Objects like porcelain cups get a million more hits of one hundredth of a pound per square inch than hits of a hundred pounds per square inch — we are necessarily immune to the cumulative effect of small deviations, which affect us disproportionally less (nonlinearly less) than larger ones
For the fragile, the cumulative effect of small shocks is smaller than the single effect of an equivalent single large shock.
  • The fragile is what is hurt a lot more by extreme events than by a succession of intermediate ones — and there is no other way to be fragile

The Antifragile Case

  • Flipping the argument: antifragility, too, is grounded in nonlinearities, nonlinear responses
For the antifragile, shocks bring more benefits (equivalently, less harm) as their intensity increases (up to a point).
  • A simple case — known heuristically by weight lifters: lifting one hundred pounds once brings more benefits than lifting fifty pounds twice, and certainly a lot more than lifting one pound a hundred times
  • Benefits in weight-lifter terms: strengthening the body, muscle mass, and bar-fight looks rather than resistance and ability to run a marathon
  • The second fifty pounds play a larger role, hence the nonlinear (i.e., convexity) effect — every additional pound brings more benefits, until one gets close to the limit, what weight lifters call “failure“
  • The reach of this simple curve affects just about anything in sight — medical error, government size, innovation — anything that touches uncertainty — and it helps put the “plumbing“ behind the statements on size and concentration

When to Smile and When to Frown

  • Nonlinearity comes in two kinds: concave (curves inward), as in the case of the king and the stone, or its opposite, convex (curves outward) — and of course, mixed, with concave and convex sections
  • The convex resembles a smile and the concave resembles a frown
  • Taleb uses the term “convexity effect“ for both, saying “positive convexity effects“ and “negative convexity effects“
  • Why does asymmetry map to convexity or concavity? If for a given variation you have more upside than downside and you draw the curve, it will be convex; the opposite for the concave
  • The convex graph turns into concave when one simply puts a minus sign in front of it — e.g., Fat Tony had the exact opposite payoff than a bank in a certain transaction: he made a buck whenever they lost one, and vice versa — profits and losses are mirror images
  • The convex likes volatility — if you earn more than you lose from fluctuations, you want a lot of fluctuations

Why Is the Concave Hurt by Black Swan Events?

  • The more concave an exposure, the more harm from the unexpected, and disproportionately so — very large deviations have a disproportionately larger and larger effect
  • Figure 13 illustrates two exposures: one linear, one nonlinear with negative convexity (concavity) in the first graph, positive convexity in the second
  • An unexpected event affects the nonlinear disproportionately more — the larger the event, the larger the difference

Traffic in New York

  • Traffic is highly nonlinear — when Taleb takes the day flight from New York to London and leaves at 5 a.m., it takes 26 minutes to reach JFK; leaving at 6 a.m. for the later flight, there is almost no difference in travel time
  • One can add more and more cars on the highway with no or minimal impact on time spent in traffic — then, a mystery: increase the number of cars by 10% and watch the travel time jump by 50%
  • The convexity effect at work: the average number of cars on the road does not matter much; what matters is the volatility of the number of cars — every additional car increases travel time more than the previous one
  • Travel cost is fragile to the volatility of the number of cars on the highway
  • This is a hint to a central problem: the misunderstanding of nonlinear response by those involved in creating “efficiencies“ and “optimization“ of systems
  • European airports and railroads are stretched, seeming overly efficient — operating at close to maximal capacity with minimal redundancies — but a small increase in congestion (say 5% more planes) can give rise to chaos in airports, with travelers camping on floors
  • Central banks can print money with no effect (and claim “safety“), then “unexpectedly“ the printing causes a jump in inflation — many economic results are completely canceled by convexity effects
  • The tools and culture of policy makers are based on the overly linear, ignoring these hidden effects — they call it “approximation“; when you hear of a “second-order“ effect, it means convexity is causing the failure of approximation

Someone Call New York City Officials

  • An illustration of how convexity effects affect an overoptimized system, along with misforecasting large deviations
  • New York City officials underestimated the effect of a line closure on traffic congestion — a small modification with compounded results in a system that is extremely stretched, hence fragile
  • Taleb drove to NYC one Saturday evening in November 2011 to meet philosopher Paul Boghossian for dinner — typically a forty-minute trip
  • He had been advocating the injection of redundancy into people’s lives and boasted of never being late to anything since his New Year’s resolution of 2007 — such discipline forces building buffers
  • The greatest benefit of never-being-late discipline: it prevents cramming his day with appointments (which are typically neither useful nor pleasant)
  • Around six o’clock, hitting Midtown, traffic stopped completely — by eight, he had moved hardly a few blocks — even his “redundancy buffer“ failed
  • What happened: NYC had authorized a film company to use the Fifty-ninth Street Bridge, blocking part of it, assuming it would be no problem on a Saturday — the small traffic problem turned into mayhem owing to the multiplicative effects — what they felt would be at worst a few minutes’ delays was multiplied by two orders of magnitude; minutes became hours
  • NYC authorities simply did not understand nonlinearities
  • This is the central problem of efficiency: these types of errors compound, multiply, swell, with an effect that only goes in one direction — the wrong direction

Where More Is Different

  • Another intuitive way to look at convexity effects: consider the scaling property — if you double the exposure, do you more than double the harm? If so, it is a situation of fragility; otherwise, you are robust
  • P. W. Anderson’s paper “More Is Different“ — what scientists in complexity call “emerging properties“ is the nonlinear result of adding units, as the sum becomes increasingly different from the parts
  • A city is not a large village; a corporation is not a larger small business — a state is not a large village — all these show nonlinearity in action

A “Balanced Meal“

  • An example of missing the hidden dimension of variability: we are told by the “Soviet-Harvard“ U.S. health authorities to eat set quantities of nutrients every day in recommended amounts — every food item has a “percentage daily allowance“
  • Aside from the total lack of empirical rigor in how these recommendations are derived, there is an insistence on regularity — getting calories and nutrients “steadily“ throughout the day with “balanced“ composition and metronomic regularity does not necessarily have the same effect as consuming them unevenly or randomly
  • This is a denial of hormesis — the slight stressor of episodic deprivation
  • For a long time, nobody even bothered to figure out whether variability in distribution — the second-order effect — mattered as much as long-term composition
  • Research now shows that the effect of variability in food sources and the nonlinearity in the physiological response is central to biological systems — consuming no protein at all on Monday and catching up on Wednesday seemingly causes a different — better — physiological response, possibly because the deprivation activates pathways that facilitate subsequent absorption
  • Until a few recent (and disconnected) empirical studies, this convexity effect had been totally missed by science — though not by religions, ancestral heuristics, and traditions
  • Even if scientists get some convexity effects in dose response, the notion of convexity effects itself appears completely missing from their language and methods

Run, Don’t Walk

  • An illustration that benefits from variation — positive convexity effects: Take two brothers, Castor and Polydeuces, who need to travel a mile
  • Castor walks the mile at a leisurely pace and arrives in twenty minutes; Polydeuces spends fourteen minutes playing with his handheld device then runs the same mile in six minutes, arriving at the same time
  • Both have covered the same distance in the same time — same average — but Castor (who walked) will presumably not get the same health benefits and gains in strength as Polydeuces (who sprinted)
  • Health benefits are convex to speed (up to a point, of course)
  • The very idea of exercise is to gain from antifragility to workout stressors — all kinds of exercise are just exploitations of convexity effects

Small May Be Ugly, It Is Certainly Less Fragile

  • We often hear “small is beautiful“ — let us present it within the approach of fragility equals concavity equals dislike of randomness

How to Be Squeezed

  • A squeeze occurs when people have no choice but to do something, and do it right away, regardless of the costs
  • Example: You need to fly to Marburg to defend a doctoral thesis, meet the parents, and get formally engaged — you buy an economy ticket to Frankfurt for $400 through London, but upon arriving at JFK, flights to London are canceled due to weather — you can get a last-minute flight to Frankfurt for $4,000, close to ten times the price — that’s a squeeze
  • Squeezes are exacerbated by size — when one is large, one becomes vulnerable to some errors, particularly horrendous squeezes — the squeezes become nonlinearly costlier as size increases
  • Why you should not own an elephant as a pet: if there is a water shortage (a squeeze), you would have to pay a higher and higher price for each additional gallon of water — that’s fragility, a negative convexity effect coming from getting too big
  • The unexpected cost, as a percentage of the total, would be monstrous — owning a cat or dog would not bring about such high unexpected additional costs at times of squeeze

Kerviel and Micro-Kerviel

  • In spite of what is studied in business schools concerning “economies of scale,“ size hurts you at times of stress — it is not a good idea to be large during difficult times
  • Some economists have been wondering why mergers of corporations do not appear to play out — the combined unit is now much larger, hence more powerful, and according to the theories of economies of scale, it should be more “efficient“ — but the numbers show, at best, no gain from such increase in size (already true in 1978)
  • Richard Roll voiced the “hubris hypothesis,“ finding it irrational for companies to engage in mergers given their poor historical record — recent data, more than three decades later, still confirm the poor record of mergers and the same hubris
  • There appears to be something about size that is harmful to corporations
  • As with elephants as pets, squeezes are much more expensive (relative to size) for large corporations — gains from size are visible but the risks are hidden, and some concealed risks seem to bring frailties into the companies
  • Large animals (elephants, boa constrictors, mammoths) and other animals of size tend to become rapidly extinct — aside from the squeeze when resources are tight, there are mechanical considerations — large animals are more fragile to shocks than small ones — again, stone and pebbles
  • Jared Diamond, always ahead of others, figured out such vulnerability in a paper called “Why Cats Have Nine Lives“ — if you throw a cat or a mouse from an elevation of several times their height, they will typically manage to survive; elephants, by comparison, break limbs very easily

The Socit Gnrale Fire Sale

  • Socit Gnrale had to sell seventy billion dollars’ worth of stocks in a single “fire sale“ — markets were not very active (“thin“) as it was Martin Luther King Day in the United States
  • Markets worldwide dropped precipitously, close to 10 percent, costing the company close to six billion dollars in losses just from their fire sale
  • They couldn’t wait — over the weekend they had uncovered a fraud by Jerome Kerviel, a rogue back office employee playing with humongous sums in the market and hiding exposures from the main computer system
  • They had no choice but to sell immediately — stocks they didn’t even know they owned

Fragility from Size (Figure 15)

  • Transaction costs as a function of size increase nonlinearly — this demonstrates megafragility
  • A fire sale of $70 billion worth of stocks leads to a loss of $6 billion, but a fire sale a tenth of that size ($7 billion) would result in no loss at all, as markets would absorb it without panic
  • If instead of one very large bank with Kerviel as rogue trader, there were ten smaller banks each with a proportional “Micro-Kerviel“ conducting rogue trading independently at random times, total losses for the ten banks would be close to nothing

Taleb’s Presentation to Socit Gnrale

  • A few weeks before the Kerviel episode, a French business school hired Taleb to present to the board of executives of the Socit Gnrale meeting in Prague about Black Swan risks
  • In the eyes of the bankers, he was like a Jesuit preacher visiting Mecca in the middle of the annual Hajj — their “quants“ and risk people hated him with passion
  • His talk was about pseudo risk techniques la Triffat — methods commonly used to measure and predict events that have never worked before, and how they needed to focus on fragility and barbells
  • He was heckled relentlessly by Kerviel’s boss and the head of risk management; after his talk everyone ignored him as if he were a Martian
  • The only person who was nice was the chairman, who mistook him for someone else and had no clue about what he was discussing
  • Shortly after returning to New York, the Kerviel scandal broke — it was tantalizing that he had to keep his mouth shut for legal reasons

The Real Problem Is Size

  • The postmortem analyses were mistaken — attributing the problem to bad controls, the bad capitalistic system, and lack of vigilance
  • It was not “greed“ as commonly assumed — the problem is primarily size, and the fragility that comes from size
  • Always keep in mind the difference between a stone and its weight in pebbles — the Kerviel story is illustrative and generalizable across domains

Project Size and Fragility

  • In project management, Bent Flyvbjerg has shown firm evidence that an increase in the size of projects maps to poor outcomes and higher costs of delays as a proportion of the total budget
  • A nuance: it is the size per segment of the project that matters, not the entire project — some projects can be divided into pieces, not others
  • Bridge and tunnel projects involve monolithic planning — cannot be broken into small portions; their percentage cost overruns increase markedly with size (same with dams)
  • For roads, built by small segments, there is no serious size effect — project managers incur only small errors and can adapt; small segments go one small error at a time, with no serious role for squeezes

Large Corporations Endangering Neighborhoods

  • Large corporations end up endangering neighborhoods — Taleb argues against large superstore chains in spite of advertised benefits
  • A large super-megastore wanted to acquire an entire neighborhood near where he lives — the argument in favor was “revitalization“
  • His counter-argument: should the company go bust (which statistically it eventually will), the neighborhood becomes a massive war zone
  • This is the argument British advisors Rohan Silva and Steve Hilton have used in favor of small merchants, along with the poetic “small is beautiful“
  • It is completely wrong to use the calculus of benefits without including the probability of failure

How to Exit a Movie Theater

  • Another example of fragility from size: imagine how people exit a movie theater when someone shouts “fire“ — a dozen persons squashed to death
  • The fragility of the theater is linked to size — every additional person exiting brings more and more trauma (a negative convexity effect)
  • A thousand people exiting in one minute is not the same as the same number exiting in half an hour
  • Someone unfamiliar with the business who naively optimizes the size of the place (like Heathrow airport) might miss that smooth functioning at regular times is different from rough functioning at times of stress
  • Contemporary economic optimized life causes us to build larger and larger theaters but with the exact same door — we no longer make this mistake with cinemas and stadiums, but we do in other domains like natural resources and food supplies
  • The price of wheat more than tripled in 20042007 in response to a small increase in net demand, around 1 percent
Bottlenecks are the mothers of all squeezes.

Projects and Prediction

Why Planes Don’t Arrive Early

  • Travelers do not like uncertainty — especially when on a set schedule — there is a one-way effect
  • Taleb has taken the LondonNew York flight most of his life (about seven hours) — he recalls a few instances of arriving early (about twenty minutes, no more), but there have been instances of arriving two or three hours late, and at least one instance of it taking more than two days to reach his destination
  • Because travel time cannot be really negative, uncertainty tends to cause delays — making arrival time increase, almost never decrease
  • Arrival time may decrease by just minutes, but increase by hours — an obvious asymmetry
  • Anything unexpected — any shock, any volatility — is much more likely to extend the total flying time
  • This also explains the irreversibility of time — the passage of time as an increase in disorder

Applying to Projects

  • Just as when you add uncertainty to a flight, planes tend to land later, not earlier — when you add uncertainty to projects, they tend to cost more and take longer to complete
  • These laws of physics are so universal they even work in Russia
  • The interpretation in the past was that a psychological bias — the underestimation of the random structure of the world — was the cause (projects take longer because estimates are too optimistic)
  • This is called overconfidence — decision scientists and business psychologists theorized the “planning fallacy“ to explain why projects take longer, rarely less time, using psychological factors

Historical Projects and the Planning Fallacy

  • The puzzle: such underestimation did not seem to exist in the past century or so, though humans had the same biases
  • Many large-scale projects a century and a half ago were completed on time — many tall buildings and monuments we see today are not just more elegant than modernistic structures but were completed within, and often ahead of, schedule
  • Examples: the Empire State Building (still standing in New York), the London Crystal Palace (erected for the Great Exhibition of 1851, based on inventive ideas of a gardener, went from concept to grand opening in just nine months — 1,848 feet long by 454 feet wide, constructed from cast iron frames and glass)
  • The Crystal Palace project did not use computers, parts were built close to the source, with a small number of businesses in the supply chain; there were no business schools teaching “project management“ and no consulting firms — the agency problem was not significant
  • It was a much more linear economy — less complex — than today’s; we have more nonlinearities, asymmetries, convexities in today’s world

Why Black Swan Effects Are Increasing

  • Black Swan effects are necessarily increasing as a result of complexity, interdependence between parts, globalization, and the beastly thing called “efficiency“ that makes people sail too close to the wind
  • Add consultants and business schools — one problem somewhere can halt the entire project — projects tend to get as weak as the weakest link in their chain (an acute negative convexity effect)
  • The world is getting less and less predictable, and we rely more on technologies that have errors and interactions that are harder to estimate
  • The information economy is the culprit — Bent Flyvbjerg showed the problem of cost overruns and delays is much more acute in the presence of information technologies (IT), as computer projects cause a large share of these cost overruns
  • The logic is simple: negative convexity effects are the main culprit, a direct and visible cause — there is an asymmetry in the way errors hit you, the same as with travel

The Planning Fallacy Is Not Psychological

  • No psychologist who has discussed the “planning fallacy“ has realized that at the core, it is not essentially a psychological problem — not an issue with human errors; it is inherent to the nonlinear structure of the projects
  • Just as time cannot be negative, a three-month project cannot be completed in zero or negative time
  • On a timeline going left to right, errors add to the right end, not the left end — if uncertainty were linear we would observe some projects completed extremely early (just as we would arrive sometimes very early, sometimes very late) — but this is not the case

Wars, Deficits, and Deficits

  • The Great War was estimated to last only a few months; by the time it was over, it had gotten France and Britain heavily in debt — they incurred at least ten times what they thought their financial costs would be
  • In the United States, the Iraq war was expected to cost thirty to sixty billion dollars; taking into account all indirect costs, it may have swelled to more than two trillion — indirect costs multiply, causing chains of interactions all going in the same direction of more costs
  • Complexity plus asymmetry (plus types like George W. Bush) once again lead to explosive errors
  • The larger the military, the disproportionally larger the cost overruns
  • Wars — with more than twentyfold errors — are only illustrative of the way governments underestimate explosive nonlinearities (convexity effects) and why they should not be trusted with finances or any large-scale decisions
  • Governments do not need wars to get us in trouble with deficits: the underestimation of costs of their projects is chronic for the very same reason 98 percent of contemporary projects have overruns
  • Taleb’s governmental golden rule: no borrowing allowed, forced fiscal balance

Where the “Efficient“ Is Not Efficient

  • The costs of fragility are swelling visibly — global disaster costs are today more than three times what they were in the 1980s, adjusting for inflation
  • The effect, noted by visionary researcher Daniel Zajdenweber, seems to be accelerating — the economy can get more and more “efficient,“ but fragility is causing the costs of errors to be higher
  • Stock exchanges have converted from “open outcry“ (wild traders face each other, yelling and screaming in a souk, then go drink together) to being replaced by computers — for very small visible benefits and massively large risks
  • While errors made by traders are confined and distributed, those made by computerized systems go wild — in August 2010, a computer error made the entire market crash (the “flash crash“); in August 2012, the Knight Capital Group had its computer system go wild and cause $10 million of losses a minute, losing $480 million
  • Naive cost-benefit analyses can be harmful — an effect that swells with size; for instance, the French focused on nuclear energy as it seemed “clean“ and cheap and “optimal“ on a computer screen, then after the Fukushima disaster of 2011 had to scramble to add safety features at any cost — a squeeze similar to those mentioned earlier
  • Additional expense was not part of the cost-benefit analysis that went into the initial decision — when comparing fuels or similar comparisons, model error may hit one side more than the other

Pollution and Harm to the Planet

  • Fossil fuels are harmful in a nonlinear way — the harm is necessarily concave (a little bit is devoid of harm, a lot can cause climatic disturbances)
  • On epistemological grounds, we do not need to believe in anthropogenic climate change — to be ecologically conservative, we can use convexity effects to produce a risk management rule for pollution
  • Simply, just as with size, split your sources of pollution among many natural sources — the harm from polluting with ten different sources is smaller than the equivalent pollution from a single source

Concentration Effects

  • Ancestral mechanisms for regulating concentration effects: contemporary humans go to stores and purchase the same items (tuna, coffee, tea, rice, mozzarella, Cabernet wine, olive oil) — items that appear not easily substitutable
  • Because of sticky contemporary habits, cultural contagion, and rigidity of factories, we are led to excessive use of specific products — this concentration is harmful
  • Extreme consumption of, say, tuna, can hurt other animals, mess with the ecosystem, and lead species to extinction — not only does the harm scale nonlinearly, but the shortages lead to disproportional rises in prices
  • Jennifer Dunne, a complexity researcher who studies hunter-gatherers, examined evidence about the Aleuts, a North American native tribe — they exhibit a remarkable lack of concentration in their predatorial behavior, with a strategy of prey switching
  • They were not as sticky and rigid as us in their habits — whenever they got low on a resource, they switched to another one, as if to preserve the ecosystem — they understood convexity effects (or rather, their habits did)
  • Globalization has made contagions planetary — the entire world became a huge room with narrow exits and people rushing to the same doors, with accelerated harm
  • Just as every child reads Harry Potter and joins Facebook, people when they get rich start to engage in the same activities and buy the same items — they drink Cabernet wine, hope to visit Venice and Florence, dream of buying a second home in the South of France — tourist locations are becoming unbearable

The Nonlinearity of Wealth

  • The fragilizing effect of contemporary globalization can be attributed to complexity, connectivity, and cultural contagions making economic gyrations much more severe — the classic switch to Extremistan
  • But there is another effect: wealth — because of nonlinear scaling, more is different
  • We are prone to make more severe errors because we are simply wealthier — just as projects of one hundred million dollars are more unpredictable and more likely to incur overruns than five-million-dollar ones
  • Simply by being richer, the world is troubled with additional unpredictability and fragility — this comes with growth, at a country level (this Highly Dreamed-of GDP Growth)
  • Even at an individual level, wealth means more headaches; we may need to work harder at mitigating the complications arising from wealth than we do at acquiring it

Chapter 18 Conclusion

  • Fragility in any domain — from a porcelain cup to an organism, to a political system, to the size of a firm, or to delays in airports — resides in the nonlinear
  • Discovery can be seen as an antideficit — think of the exact opposite of airplane delays or project overruns — something that benefits from uncertainty
  • Discovery presents the mirror image of what we saw as fragile, randomness-hating situations

Chapter 19: The Philosopher’s Stone and Its Inverse

They tell you when they are going bust — Gold is sometimes a special variety of lead
  • This chapter is a deepening of the ideas of the previous one — it will be denser and more technical, and Taleb says it should be skipped by the “enlightened reader“

How to Detect Who Will Go Bust

  • A method to detect fragility — the inverse philosopher’s stone
  • Illustrated with the story of Fannie Mae, the giant government-sponsored lending firm that collapsed leaving the United States taxpayer with hundreds of billions of dollars of losses (and still counting)
  • In 2003, Alex Berenson, a New York Times journalist, came into Taleb’s office with secret risk reports of Fannie Mae given by a defector — the report got into the guts of the methodology for risk calculation that only an insider can see
  • Fannie Mae made its own risk calculations and disclosed what it wanted to whomever it wanted — only a defector could show the guts of how risk was calculated
  • Looking at the report: a move upward in an economic variable led to massive losses, a move downward (opposite direction) led to small profits — further moves upward led to even larger additional losses and further moves downward to even smaller profits
  • It looked exactly like the story of the stone — acceleration of harm was obvious, in fact monstrous
  • Their blowup was inevitable: their exposures were severely “concave“ — similar to the graph of traffic — losses accelerate as one deviates economic variables
  • Fragility to one variable of this magnitude implies fragility to all other parameters
  • Thanks to Berenson, The New York Times presented Taleb’s concern — a smear campaign ensued, but nothing too notable
  • The key: the nonlinear is vastly more affected by extreme events — and nobody was interested in extreme events since they had a mental block against them
  • Taleb kept telling anyone who would listen, including taxi drivers, that Fannie Mae was “sitting on a barrel of dynamite“ — people kept saying his opinion was wrong and unfounded (arguing the stock was going up)
  • He also inferred that other institutions, almost all banks, were in the same situation — after checking similar institutions and seeing the problem was general, he realized a total collapse of the banking system was a certainty
  • He was so certain he could not see straight and went back to the markets to get his revenge against the turkeys, as in the scene from The Godfather (III): “Just when I thought I was out, they pull me back in.“
  • Things happened as if planned by destiny — Fannie Mae went bust, along with other banks — it just took a bit longer than expected, no big deal

Taleb’s Discovery of the Fragility-Antifragility Measure

  • The stupid part: he had not seen the link between financial and general fragility — nor did he use the term “fragility“; he didn’t look at too many porcelain cups
  • However, thanks to the episode, in the attic he had a measure for fragility, hence antifragility
  • It all boils down to: figuring out if our miscalculations or misforecasts are on balance more harmful than they are beneficial, and how accelerating the damage is
  • Exactly as in the story of the king: the damage from a ten-kilogram stone is more than twice the damage from a five-kilogram one — such accelerating damage means a large stone would eventually kill the person, and likewise a large market deviation would eventually kill the company

The Fragility (and Antifragility) Detection Heuristic

  • Once Taleb figured out that fragility was directly from nonlinearity and convexity effects, and that convexity was measurable, he got excited
  • The technique — detecting acceleration of harm — applies to anything that entails decision making under uncertainty and risk management
